Cage molecules, such as clathrates, cryptands, and carcerands, are characterized by a closed, polyhedral framework that can encapsulate guest species within their internal cavities. Their common applications include molecular encapsulation, selective ion or molecule transport, gas storage (e.g., methane hydrates), drug delivery, and as hosts in host-guest chemistry for molecular recognition and sensing.\n\nMacrocycles, on the other hand, are large ring-shaped molecules, such as crown ethers, cyclodextrins, and porphyrins. They are widely used in ion transport and separation (e.g., selective binding of metal cations), catalysis (as enzyme mimics or in supramolecular catalysis), drug delivery, and as building blocks in supramolecular assemblies.\n\nThere is significant overlap in applications, particularly in host-guest chemistry, molecular recognition, and drug delivery, where both cage molecules and macrocycles can encapsulate or bind guest species selectively. Both are also used in the design of molecular machines and sensors. However, cage molecules are more specialized for complete encapsulation and protection of guests, while macrocycles often provide selective binding without full encapsulation.\n\nIn summary, while Cage molecules, such as clathrates, cryptands, and carcerands, are characterized by a closed, polyhedral framework that can encapsulate guest species within their internal cavities. Their common applications include molecular encapsulation, selective ion or molecule transport, gas storage (e.g., methane hydrates), drug delivery, and as hosts in host-guest chemistry for molecular recognition and sensing.\n\nMacrocycles, on the other hand, are large ring-shaped molecules, such as crown ethers, cyclodextrins, and porphyrins. They are widely used in ion transport and separation (e.g., selective binding of metal cations), catalysis (as enzyme mimics or in supramolecular catalysis), drug delivery, and as building blocks in supramolecular assemblies.\n\nThere is significant overlap in applications, particularly in host-guest chemistry, molecular recognition, and drug delivery, where both cage molecules and macrocycles can encapsulate or bind guest species selectively. Both are also used in the design of molecular machines and sensors. However, cage molecules are more specialized for complete encapsulation and protection of guests, while macrocycles often provide selective binding without full encapsulation.\n\nIn summary, while both classes are central to supramolecular chemistry and share some applications, cage molecules are distinguished by their ability to fully enclose guests, making them especially useful for Their common applications include molecular encapsulation, selective ion or molecule transport, gas storage (e.g., methane hydrates), drug delivery, and as hosts in host-guest chemistry for molecular recognition and sensing.\n\nMacrocycles, on the other hand, are large ring-shaped molecules, such as crown ethers, cyclodextrins, and porphyrins. They are widely used in ion transport and separation (e.g., selective binding of metal cations), catalysis (as enzyme mimics or in supramolecular catalysis), drug delivery, and as building blocks in supramolecular assemblies.\n\nThere is significant overlap in applications, particularly in host-guest chemistry, molecular recognition, and drug delivery, where both cage molecules and macrocycles can encapsulate or bind guest species selectively. Both are also used in the design of molecular machines and sensors. However, cage molecules are more specialized for complete encapsulation and protection of guests, while macrocycles often provide selective binding without full encapsulation.\n\nIn summary, while both classes are central to supramolecular chemistry and share some applications, cage molecules are distinguished by their ability to fully enclose guests, making them especially useful for Cage molecules, such as clathrates, cryptands, and carcerands, are characterized by a closed, polyhedral framework that can encapsulate guest species within their internal cavities. Their common applications include molecular encapsulation, selective ion or molecule transport, gas storage (e.g., methane hydrates), drug delivery, and as hosts in host-guest chemistry for molecular recognition and sensing.\n\nMacrocycles, on the other hand, are large ring-shaped molecules, such as crown ethers, cyclodextrins, and porphyrins. Both are also used in the design of molecular machines and sensors. However, cage molecules are more specialized for complete encapsulation and protection of guests, while macrocycles often provide selective binding without full encapsulation.\n\nIn summary, while
